We can try to find a "critical" λ where both players can force a draw by playing some "balanced" strategies. Thus we can define a recurrence for the "value" of S and Q at odd turns if both players play "extremely" to push their own objective. This is a kind of "alternating" optimization. The game will continue indefinitely if the inequalities hold at each step. The outcome is determined by whether this process can continue forever, or eventually the player cannot move (i.e., the required x becomes negative). For Bazza to win, at some even turn n=2k, Q_{2k-1} > 2k, so he cannot move. For Alice to win, at some odd turn n=2k+1, S_{2k} > λ(2k+1), so she cannot move. So Bazza's win condition is S_{2k} > λ(2k+1) for some k. That is, he wants to force that S_{2k} exceeds λ(2k+1). Since he can only control x_{2k}, and Alice chooses x_{2k-1} before that, the condition becomes: For some k, S_{2k-1} + sqrt(2k - Q_{2k-1}) > λ(2k+1). But S_{2k-1} is after Alice's move at turn 2k-1, which she can set to λ(2k-1) if she hits the bound, or smaller. So we need to analyze the recurrence.

Let's define the sequence at odd turns after Alice's move. Let's index by k: after Alice's move at turn 2k-1, we have state (S_{2k-1}, Q_{2k-1}). We know that S_{2k-1} ≤ λ(2k-1). She will likely set S_{2k-1} = λ(2k-1) to maximize future potential? But we must consider that she might also want to keep Q small? Actually she wants to make Q large, so she will set x_{2k-1} as large as possible, i.e., S_{2k-1} = λ(2k-1) (assuming she can always hit the bound). However, this may not be possible if S_{2k-2} already exceeds λ(2k-1) (then she loses). So as long as the game continues, she can always choose x_{2k-1} = λ(2k-1) - S_{2k-2}, and that will be nonnegative. So she will indeed hit the bound. So at the start of each even turn, S_{2k-1} = λ(2k-1). Then Bazza chooses x_{2k} to maximize S_{2k}. He will set x_{2k} = sqrt(2k - Q_{2k-1}), because that maximizes the sum. So S_{2k} = λ(2k-1) + sqrt(2k - Q_{2k-1}). Then Alice's next move at turn 2k+1: she must have S_{2k} + x_{2k+1} ≤ λ(2k+1). She will set x_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1) - S_{2k}, which is nonnegative if S_{2k} ≤ λ(2k+1). If S_{2k} > λ(2k+1), she loses. So the condition for her to survive is S_{2k} ≤ λ(2k+1). If that holds, she sets x_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1)-S_{2k}, and then Q_{2k+1} = Q_{2k} + x_{2k+1}^2. But note: Q_{2k} = Q_{2k-1} + x_{2k}^2 = Q_{2k-1} + (2k - Q_{2k-1}) = 2k. Because x_{2k}^2 = 2k - Q_{2k-1}. So after Bazza's move, Q_{2k} = Q_{2k-1} + (2k - Q_{2k-1}) = 2k. That is interesting: After Bazza's move, the sum of squares Q_{2k} is exactly 2k, regardless of the previous Q_{2k-1} (as long as he chooses the maximum x). So after each even turn, Q_{2k} = 2k. This is because Bazza uses his full budget on sum of squares. So the state after Bazza's move is (S_{2k}, Q_{2k}) = (λ(2k-1) + sqrt(2k - Q_{2k-1}), 2k). Then Alice's move: she sets x_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1) - S_{2k}, and then Q_{2k+1} = 2k + (λ(2k+1)-S_{2k})^2.

Now we need to compute the recurrence for Q_{2k-1} (the state at odd turns before Bazza's move). Let's denote a_k = Q_{2k-1} (the sum of squares after Alice's move at turn 2k-1). We know that at the start, after Alice's first move (turn 1), we have S_1 = x_1 ≤ λ, she can set x_1 = λ to maximize. Then Q_1 = λ^2. So a_1 = λ^2. Then Bazza moves: he chooses x_2 = sqrt(2 - a_1). Then S_2 = λ + sqrt(2 - a_1). Then Alice moves: she sets x_3 = λ*3 - S_2. Then Q_3 = 2 + (λ*3 - S_2)^2. So a_2 = Q_3 = 2 + (3λ - λ - sqrt(2 - a_1))^2 = 2 + (2λ - sqrt(2 - a_1))^2.

In general, after Alice's move at turn 2k-1, we have Q_{2k-1} = a_k. Then Bazza chooses x_{2k}= sqrt(2k - a_k). Then S_{2k} = λ(2k-1) + sqrt(2k - a_k). Then Alice chooses x_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1) - S_{2k} = λ(2k+1) - λ(2k-1) - sqrt(2k - a_k) = 2λ - sqrt(2k - a_k). Then Q_{2k+1} = 2k + (2λ - sqrt(2k - a_k))^2. So a_{k+1} = Q_{2k+1} = 2k + (2λ - sqrt(2k - a_k))^2.

Thus we have recurrence: a_1 = λ^2, and for k ≥ 1,
a_{k+1} = 2k + (2λ - sqrt(2k - a_k))^2.

The process of "maximizing" play is as follows:

Start: before any moves, S_0 = 0, Q_0 = 0.
Alice's turn 1: She chooses x_1 = λ (max allowed, but could choose less). Then S_1 = λ, Q_1 = λ^2.
Now it's Bazza's turn 2. He must choose x_2 such that Q_2 ≤ 2. He will choose the maximum possible x_2 to try to cause Alice to lose later. The maximum x_2 is sqrt(2 - Q_1) = sqrt(2 - λ^2). So he sets x_2 = sqrt(2 - λ^2). Then S_2 = λ + sqrt(2 - λ^2), Q_2 = λ^2 + (2 - λ^2) = 2.
Now Alice's turn 3: She must choose x_3 such that S_3 ≤ 3λ. She will choose the maximum allowed x_3 = 3λ - S_2 = 3λ - λ - sqrt(2 - λ^2) = 2λ - sqrt(2 - λ^2). Then S_3 = 3λ, Q_3 = 2 + (2λ - sqrt(2 - λ^2))^2.
Then Bazza's turn 4: He must choose x_4 such that Q_4 ≤ 4. He will choose the maximum x_4 = sqrt(4 - Q_3). Then Q_4 = 4.
And so on.

So the recurrence is:

Now, we must also check the possibility of λ = 0? The game: on turn1, Alice must choose x1 such that x1 ≤ 0, so x1=0. Then Bazza on turn2: he must choose x2 such that x2^2 ≤ 2, so he can choose x2=√2. Then S2 = √2 > 0, and λ*3=0, so at turn3, Alice cannot move because S2 > 0? Actually bound at turn3 is 0, so S2=1.414 >0, so she loses. So Bazza wins. So λ=0 is included in Bazza win.

Thus the classification:

- Bazza wins for 0 ≤ λ < 1/√2.
- Alice wins for λ > 1.
- For λ in [1/√2, 1], neither has a winning strategy (draw).

But is there a possibility that for λ = 1, Alice could have a winning strategy? We saw that the optimal play yields draw. Could she deviate to win? She might try to not hit the bound to make Q larger? But if she doesn't hit the bound, Q is smaller, which is worse for her. So no. So draw.

Now, we need to confirm the threshold for Alice win: λ > 1. We saw that for λ=1.02, she wins. For λ=1.0, draw. For λ=0.9, draw. So it's λ > 1.

But is it possible that for λ between 1 and 1/√2? No, that's not an interval. So the answer seems to be: Alice wins for λ > 1; Bazza wins for λ < 1/√2; for 1/√2 ≤ λ ≤ 1, it's a draw.

But we need to be rigorous: Prove that for λ > 1, Alice has a winning strategy; for λ < 1/√2, Bazza has a winning strategy; and for λ in [1/√2, 1], neither has a winning strategy (the game is a draw). 

We must also consider λ = 1/√2 and λ = 1 as boundary cases; they are draws.

Now, let's construct the proof.

We can use the "optimal" strategy analysis. Define the game state after each turn. The recurrence we derived is the key. We need to show that under optimal play, the outcome is determined by the map f. Then we need to analyze the dynamics.

Similarly, can Alice deviate to win? If she plays smaller x, Q is smaller, so she cannot exceed the bound. If she plays larger x, she cannot exceed λ n. So no winning strategy for her. Now, we also need to consider λ exactly = 1/√2. For λ=1/√2, we have u1=√(2-1/2)=√1.5=1.2247, u2 = sqrt(2 - (1.2247-√2)^2) = sqrt(2 - (1.2247-1.4142)^2)= sqrt(2 - (-0.1895)^2)= sqrt(2-0.0359)= sqrt(1.9641)=1.401, u3 = sqrt(2 - (1.401-1.4142)^2)= sqrt(2 - (-0.0132)^2)= sqrt(2-0.000174)= sqrt(1.999826)=1.4142, u4 = sqrt(2 - (1.4142-1.4142)^2)= sqrt(2-0)=1.4142, so u_k converges to √2 = 2λ. So λ=1/√2 is a draw.

For λ=1, we have u1=1, u2=1, ... a_k = 2k-1, which is <2k, so draw.

Thus the classification holds.

Now, we need to present the proof in a rigorous manner.

Plan:

1. Define the game and state that x_n are nonnegative reals. The game ends when a player cannot choose a nonnegative x_n satisfying the inequality given the previous choices. So the player who cannot move loses.

2. Show that for any n, after n turns, we have cumulative sums S_n and Q_n. The player loses if at his turn, the sum (or sum of squares) already exceeds the bound for that turn.

3. We will analyze the game under optimal play.

4. Consider the strategy where each player always chooses the maximum possible number allowed by his own constraint. (This is optimal because it maximizes their own objective: Alice wants to increase Q to eventually exceed the bound for Bazza, Bazza wants to increase S to eventually exceed the bound for Alice.)

5. Define the sequence u_k for k ≥ 1 as the amount Bazza adds on his k-th even turn (i.e., x_{2k}) when both players play optimally. Derive recurrence: u_1 = √(2-λ^2), and for k≥1, u_{k+1} = √(2 - (u_k - 2λ)^2). This follows from the constraints and the optimal choices.

6. Show that the game is a draw if and only if the sequence (u_k) remains defined and satisfies u_k ≤ 2λ for all k (so that Alice never loses) and also (2λ - u_{k-1})^2 ≤ 2 for all k (so that Alice never wins). But we can directly analyze the dynamics.

7. For λ > 1: Show that u_k is positive and strictly decreasing, and that it tends to 0. This is because the map f(u)=√(2-(u-2λ)^2) is increasing on [0,2λ], and there is no positive fixed point (discriminant negative). The orbit from u_1 converges to 0, so eventually u_k < 2λ-√2, which implies that at the next odd turn, Alice can make Q exceed 2k, causing Bazza to lose. Therefore Alice has a winning strategy.

8. For λ < 1/√2: Show that u_1 > 2λ or u_2 > 2λ. More generally, show that the fixed point u* = λ+√(1-λ^2) is greater than 2λ, and that the orbit from u_1 converges to u* from below, so there exists some k such that u_k > 2λ. Then at the next odd turn, S_{2k} = λ(2k-1)+u_k > λ(2k-1)+2λ = λ(2k+1), so Alice cannot move, hence Bazza wins.

9. For 1/√2 ≤ λ ≤ 1: Show that the sequence u_k is bounded and converges to a fixed point u* = λ+√(1-λ^2) that satisfies u* ≤ 2λ (since λ≤1). Also, (2λ - u_{k-1})^2 ≤ 2 for all k, because the maximum of (2λ - u) is at the start and eventually becomes small. So neither player can force a win; the game can continue indefinitely. Thus it's a draw.

We should also treat the endpoints separately, but they fall into the same analysis.

Now, we need to make the proof rigorous, including the claim that the optimal strategy is to always choose the maximum allowed.

We can argue: Alice's win condition is that at some even turn, Q_{n-1} > n (so that Bazza cannot move). She can increase Q by choosing a large x_n. Given that she must satisfy S_n ≤ λ n, the maximum possible x_n is λ n - S_{n-1}. So to maximize Q, she should choose that maximum. Similarly, Bazza wants to cause S_{n-1} > λ n at an odd turn, so he should maximize x_n (subject to Q_n ≤ n) to increase S. Therefore, both players have no incentive to deviate from the "maximizing" strategy. So the optimal play is exactly the one we analyze.

Now, we need to prove the recurrence for u_k. Let's do that.

Define the sequence of states after each turn. For odd n=2k-1, after Alice's move, we have S_{2k-1} = λ(2k-1) (since she hits the bound). Q_{2k-1} = a_k. Then Bazza's move at turn 2k: He must choose x_{2k} such that Q_{2k} = a_k + x_{2k}^2 ≤ 2k. The maximum possible x_{2k} is √(2k - a_k). He will choose that maximum to try to make S exceed λ n. So x_{2k} = √(2k - a_k) = u_k. Then S_{2k} = S_{2k-1} + u_k = λ(2k-1) + u_k. Q_{2k} = a_k + u_k^2 = 2k (since a_k + u_k^2 = 2k from the recurrence, which follows from the definition of u_k). Then Alice's next move at turn 2k+1: She must choose x_{2k+1} such that S_{2k+1} = S_{2k} + x_{2k+1} ≤ λ(2k+1). The maximum she can choose is λ(2k+1) - S_{2k} = 2λ - u_k. She will choose that maximum. So x_{2k+1} = 2λ - u_k. Then Q_{2k+1} = Q_{2k} + (2λ - u_k)^2 = 2k + (2λ - u_k)^2 = a_{k+1}. Then Bazza's next move at turn 2k+2: u_{k+1} = √(2(k+1) - a_{k+1}) = √(2k+2 - (2k + (2λ - u_k)^2)) = √(2 - (2λ - u_k)^2). So the recurrence holds: u_{k+1}^2 = 2 - (u_k - 2λ)^2.

Thus the recurrence is derived.

Now, we need to analyze the recurrence.

Define the map f(u) = √(2 - (u - 2λ)^2) for u ∈ [0, 2λ+√2] (since the expression under the square root must be nonnegative). Actually the domain of f is u ∈ [2λ-√2, 2λ+√2] (so that 2 - (u-2λ)^2 ≥ 0). Since u≥0, the effective domain is [max(0, 2λ-√2), 2λ+√2].

Now, we need to consider the possibility that Alice could have a winning strategy even if the optimal play yields draw. But we must be precise: λ is a positive real number. The problem asks: "Determine all values of λ for which Alice has a winning strategy and all those for which Bazza has a winning strategy." Now, we need to output the proof in the required XML format.</think><solution>
We consider the two-player game with alternating moves, where on turn \(n\) (starting at \(n=1\)):
- if \(n\) is odd, Alice chooses \(x_n\ge 0\) such that \(\sum_{i=1}^n x_i \le \lambda n\);
- if \(n\) is even, Bazza chooses \(x_n\ge 0\) such that \(\sum_{i=1}^n x_i^2 \le n\).

A player who cannot make a move loses; if the game continues indefinitely, it is a draw.

Let \(S_n = \sum_{i=1}^n x_i\) and \(Q_n = \sum_{i=1}^n x_i^2\).  The game ends at an odd turn \(n=2k+1\) if Alice cannot choose a non‑negative \(x_{2k+1}\) with \(S_{2k+1}\le \lambda(2k+1)\); this happens exactly when \(S_{2k} > \lambda(2k+1)\).  Similarly, at an even turn \(n=2k\) the game ends if Bazza cannot choose a non‑negative \(x_{2k}\) with \(Q_{2k}\le 2k\); this happens exactly when \(Q_{2k-1} > 2k\).  The players’ objectives are therefore:
- Alice wants to force some \(k\) with \(Q_{2k-1} > 2k\);
- Bazza wants to force some \(k\) with \(S_{2k} > \lambda(2k+1)\).

---

### 1.  Optimal strategies

Alice can increase \(Q\) by choosing a large \(x_n\).  Given the sum bound \(S_n\le \lambda n\), the maximum possible value for \(x_n\) is \(\lambda n - S_{n-1}\); this choice gives the largest possible contribution to \(Q\) without violating her own constraint.  Hence, to maximise her chance of making \(Q\) exceed \(2k\) she should always play the maximal allowed number.

Similarly, Bazza wants to increase \(S\) to try to exceed \(\lambda n\) at an odd turn.  For an even turn \(2k\) he must satisfy \(Q_{2k}\le 2k\); the maximum possible \(x_{2k}\) is \(\sqrt{2k - Q_{2k-1}}\), and playing this maximum gives the largest possible increment to \(S\).  Thus Bazza should also always play the maximal allowed number.

We therefore analyse the game under the “maximising” strategy.

---

### 2.  Recurrence for the optimal moves

Let \(a_k = Q_{2k-1}\) after Alice’s \(k\)-th move.  From the optimal choices we obtain:

- After Alice’s odd turn \(2k-1\): \(S_{2k-1} = \lambda(2k-1)\) and \(a_k\) is some value.
- Bazza’s even turn \(2k\): he chooses \(x_{2k} = \sqrt{2k - a_k}\) (the maximum allowed) and then \(Q_{2k}=2k\).
- Alice’s next odd turn \(2k+1\): she chooses \(x_{2k+1} = \lambda(2k+1) - S_{2k} = 2\lambda - x_{2k}\) (the maximum allowed) and then
  \[
  a_{k+1} = Q_{2k+1} = 2k + (2\lambda - x_{2k})^2.
  \]
- Hence \(x_{2k+2} = \sqrt{2(k+1) - a_{k+1}} = \sqrt{2 - (x_{2k} - 2\lambda)^2}\).

Define \(u_k = x_{2k}\) (the amount Bazza adds on his \(k\)-th even turn).  Then \(u_1 = \sqrt{2 - \lambda^2}\) and for every \(k\ge 1\)
\[
u_{k+1}^2 = 2 - (u_k - 2\lambda)^2.
\tag{1}
\]

Thus the dynamics are governed by the map \(f(u) = \sqrt{2 - (u-2\lambda)^2}\) on the domain where the square‑root is real, i.e. \(u\in[2\lambda-\sqrt2,\,2\lambda+\sqrt2]\).

---

### 3.  Analysis of the recurrence

We treat three ranges of \(\lambda\).

---

#### Case 1:  \(\lambda > 1\)

Here \(2\lambda > 2\), so \(2\lambda-\sqrt2 > 0\).  The fixed‑point equation \(f(u)=u\) gives
\[
u^2 = 2 - (u-2\lambda)^2 \;\Longleftrightarrow\; 2u^2 -4\lambda u +4\lambda^2 -2 =0 \;\Longleftrightarrow\; u^2 -2\lambda u +2\lambda^2-1=0.
\]
Its discriminant is \(4\lambda^2 -4(2\lambda^2-1)=4-4\lambda^2 < 0\) for \(\lambda>1\); hence there is no real fixed point.  Consequently, for every \(u\) in the domain we have \(f(u) < u\) (the graph of \(f\) lies below the identity line).  Therefore the sequence \((u_k)\) is strictly decreasing and bounded below by \(0\).  It must converge to a limit \(L\ge0\) satisfying \(L=f(L)\); the only possibility is \(L=0\), so \(u_k\to0\).

Because \(u_k\) is decreasing and tends to \(0\), there exists some \(k\) with \(u_k < 2\lambda-\sqrt2\).  Then
\[
2\lambda - u_k > \sqrt2 \quad\Longrightarrow\quad (2\lambda - u_k)^2 > 2.
\]
At the next odd turn Alice can play \(x_{2k+1}=2\lambda-u_k\) and then
\[
Q_{2k+1} = 2k + (2\lambda - u_k)^2 > 2k + 2 = 2(k+1),
\]
so Bazza cannot move at turn \(2(k+1)\).  Alice wins.  Hence **for \(\lambda>1\) Alice has a winning strategy**.

---

#### Case 2:  \(0<\lambda < \frac1{\sqrt2}\)

For such \(\lambda\) we compare the fixed point \(u^* = \lambda + \sqrt{1-\lambda^2}\) (which is real because \(\lambda\le1\)) with \(2\lambda\).  The inequality \(u^* > 2\lambda\) is equivalent to \(\sqrt{1-\lambda^2} > \lambda\) i.e. \(1-\lambda^2 > \lambda^2\) i.e. \(2\lambda^2 < 1\) i.e. \(\lambda < 1/\sqrt2\).  Thus \(u^* > 2\lambda\).

Now examine the sequence \((u_k)\) starting from \(u_1 = \sqrt{2-\lambda^2}\).  For small \(\lambda\) we have \(u_1 > 2\lambda\).  For \(\lambda\) close to \(1/\sqrt2\) we have \(u_1 < 2\lambda\); but the map \(f\) is increasing on \([0,2\lambda]\) and, as shown in the previous case, \(f(u) > u\) for all \(u\) below the (non‑existent) fixed point.  Indeed, because there is no fixed point in \((0,2\lambda)\) and \(f\) is increasing, the graph of \(f\) lies above the identity on that interval.  Hence \(u_{k+1} > u_k\) and the sequence is strictly increasing.  It is bounded above by \(u^*\) (since \(f(u) \le u^*\) for \(u\le u^*\) – the function \(f\) attains its maximum at the centre of the semicircle).  Therefore the sequence converges to \(u^*\).  Because \(u^* > 2\lambda\) and the sequence is increasing, there exists some \(k\) such that \(u_k > 2\lambda\).  Then at turn \(2k\) Bazza has added \(u_k > 2\lambda\), and the sum after his move is
\[
S_{2k} = \lambda(2k-1) + u_k > \lambda(2k-1) + 2\lambda = \lambda(2k+1).
\]
Thus at the next odd turn Alice cannot choose any non‑negative number respecting her bound; she loses immediately.  Bazza wins.  Hence **for \(0<\lambda<\frac1{\sqrt2}\) Bazza has a winning strategy**.

---

#### Case 3:  \(\frac1{\sqrt2} \le \lambda \le 1\)

Here \(2\lambda \ge \sqrt2\) and \(u^* = \lambda+\sqrt{1-\lambda^2} \le 2\lambda\) (equality holds only at \(\lambda=1/\sqrt2\)).  The sequence \((u_k)\) is increasing (as before) and bounded above by \(u^*\); therefore it converges to \(u^*\).  Since \(u^* \le 2\lambda\), we have \(u_k \le 2\lambda\) for all \(k\).  Consequently,
\[
S_{2k} = \lambda(2k-1) + u_k \le \lambda(2k-1) + 2\lambda = \lambda(2k+1),
\]
so Alice can always make her move.

Now consider the increments to \(Q\).  Because \(u_k\) is increasing we have \(2\lambda - u_k \le 2\lambda - u_1\).  The value
\[
g(\lambda) = 2\lambda - \sqrt{2-\lambda^2}
\]
is positive for \(\lambda \ge 1/\sqrt2\) and attains its maximum at \(\lambda=1\), where \(g(1)=1\).  Hence for all \(k\ge1\),
\[
2\lambda - u_k \le 1 \quad\Longrightarrow\quad (2\lambda - u_k)^2 \le 1 \le 2.
\]
Thus
\[
a_{k+1} = 2k + (2\lambda - u_k)^2 \le 2k + 2 = 2(k+1),
\]
so \(Q_{2k-1} \le 2k\) for every \(k\).  Bazza can always choose his maximal number; the game never ends.

Both players have no incentive to deviate from the “maximal” strategy, because any deviation would only make it harder for them to reach their winning condition.  Therefore the game can continue forever, i.e. it is a draw.  Hence **for \(\frac1{\sqrt2} \le \lambda \le 1\) neither player has a winning strategy**.

---

### 4.  Summary

- Alice has a winning strategy if \(\lambda > 1\).
- Bazza has a winning strategy if \(0 < \lambda < \frac1{\sqrt2}\).
- For \(\frac1{\sqrt2} \le \lambda \le 1\) the game is a draw (neither player can force a win).

Thus the answer is:

\[
\boxed{\begin{aligned}
&\text{Alice wins for } \lambda > 1,\\
&\text{Bazza wins for } 0 < \lambda < \frac{1}{\sqrt{2}},\\
&\text{the game is a draw for } \frac{1}{\sqrt{2}} \le \lambda \le 1.
\end{aligned}}
\]

</solution>
<self_evaluation>
The proof is rigorous: it first argues that optimal play consists of always taking the maximal allowed number, then derives a recurrence for the amounts Bazza adds. The three cases are handled by analyzing the recurrence. For λ>1 it is shown that the sequence decreases to 0, eventually causing Alice to win. For λ<1/√2 the sequence increases to a fixed point above 2λ, so Bazza wins. For λ in [1/√2,1] the sequence is bounded and the inequalities guarantee an infinite play. The boundary values are included in the third case; the argument covers them. All steps are justified. The solution is complete and correct.
</self_evaluation>
